Problem: What is the sum of all integers $x$ for which $-30 \leq x \leq 26$ ? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 0 \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: The sum of all integers $y$ for which $-26\le y\le26$ is 0 since for every negative term there is a positive term of the same absolute value. Thus, the sum of all integers $x$ for which $-30\le x\le26$ is $-30-29-28-27=-30\times4+1+2+3=-120+6=\boxed{-114}$.

Problem: If the digits represented by $A$ and $B$ satisfy the following subtraction problem $$ \begin{array}{c@{}c@{\;}c@{}c@{}c@{}c} & & & & A & B_4\\ &- & & & B & A_4\\ \cline{2-6} & & & & & 3_4\\ \end{array} $$what is the nonnegative difference of the digits represented by $A$ and $B$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 3 \boxed{3}

Reference Answer: In the subtraction problem, we can start by looking at the left column. The first possible case is that $A-B=0$, so that $A=B$. However, if $A=B$, that does not work in the right column, since $B-A$ would equal 0 and not 3. So, we have to go with the second possibility for the left column, in which the right column borrowed from the left column and we're left with $(A-1)-B=0$. This tells us that $A-1=B$, so the nonnegative difference of $A$ and $B$ is $\boxed{1}$. Note that we are unable to solve for the actual digits $A$ and $B$ even if we consider the units digit. Now that we know the right column borrows from the left column, we get that $$ \begin{array}{c@{}c@{\;}c@{}c@{}c@{}c} & & & & 1 & B_4\\ &- & & & & A_4\\ \cline{2-6} & & & & & 3_4.\\ \end{array} $$This also means $A+3=1B_4=4+B$, which is the same as $A-1=B$. We can't solve for $A$ and $B$ because any digits that satisfy $A-1=B$ will work, such as $A=3, B=2$ or $A=2, B=1$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Now that we know the right column borrows from the left column, we get that $$ \begin{array}{c@{}c@{\;}c@{}c@{}c@{}c} & & & & 1 & B_4\\ &- & & & & A_4\\ \cline{2-6} & & & & & 3_4.\\ \end{array} $$This also means $A+3=1B_4=4+B$, which is the same as $A-1=B$. We can't solve for $A$ and $B$ because any digits that satisfy $A-1=B$ will work, such as $A=3, B=2$ or $A=2, B=1$.

Problem: Determine whether the sequence \(y_n = \left(1 - \frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}},\dots,\frac{1}{2^{2n}},0,0,0,\dots\right)\) converges in \(\ell^1\) to \((1,0,0,0,\dots)\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the sequence \( y_n = \left(1 - \frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \) converges in \( \ell^1 \) to \( x = (1, 0, 0, 0, \dots) \). Hmm, let me recall what convergence in \( \ell^1 \) means. In \( \ell^1 \) space, a sequence \( (y_n) \) converges to \( x \) if the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) goes to zero as \( n \) approaches infinity. First, let's write out what \( y_n - x \) looks like. The first term of \( y_n \) is \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), so subtracting the first term of \( x \), which is 1, gives \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, the next terms of \( y_n \) are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \), followed by zeros. So, subtracting \( x \) which has all zeros after the first term, the difference \( y_n - x \) is the sequence: \[ \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Now, to find the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of this difference, we need to take the absolute value of each component and sum them up. Since all terms except the first are positive in \( y_n \), the absolute values will just be the terms themselves. Let's compute that: The first term contributes \( \left| -\frac{1}{2^n} \right| = \frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, starting from the second term up to the \( (2n - n + 1) \)-th term? Wait, let's see. The sequence \( y_n \) has non-zero entries from position 1 up to position \( 2n - n + 1 \)? Wait, maybe I need to check how many terms there are. The original \( y_n \) has entries starting from the first component \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), then the next components are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Let's count how many non-zero terms there are after the first component. The exponents in the denominators go from \( n+1 \) up to \( 2n \), inclusive. So, the number of terms is \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \). Wait, let's verify that. If you have exponents from \( n+1 \) to \( 2n \), that's \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \) terms. So, starting from the second component (position 2) up to position \( n + 1 \), there are \( n \) terms each of the form \( \frac{1}{2^{n + k}} \) where \( k \) goes from 1 to \( n \). So, in total, the non-zero entries of \( y_n - x \) are the first component \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \) and then the next \( n \) components \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) up to \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] Now, let's compute this sum. The first term is \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), and then the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( \frac{1}{2^k} \). Let's handle the summation part first. The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a finite geometric series. The general formula for the sum of a geometric series from \( k = a \) to \( k = b \) is \( \frac{r^a - r^{b+1}}{1 - r} \), where \( r \) is the common ratio. Here, \( r = \frac{1}{2} \), so the sum becomes: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1} }{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{ \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} }{ \frac{1}{2} } = 2 \left( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} \right ) = \frac{1}{2^{n}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] So, the summation part simplifies to \( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the total \( \ell^1 \)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right ) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Now, we need to evaluate the limit as \( n \) approaches infinity of this expression. Let's compute each term: First, \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} = 2 \cdot \frac{1}{2^n} \), which clearly approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Second, \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \left( \frac{1}{4} \right)^n \), which also approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the entire expression \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approaches \( 0 - 0 = 0 \). Hence, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), which means that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). Wait a second, let me double-check my steps to make sure I didn't make a mistake. So, first, the difference sequence: subtracting \( x = (1, 0, 0, \dots) \) from \( y_n \), which has first term \( 1 - 1/2^n \), so difference first term is \( -1/2^n \). Then, the next terms are \( 1/2^{n+1}, 1/2^{n+2}, ..., 1/2^{2n} \), which are all positive. Then, zeros. So, yes, the norm is the sum of absolute values of these terms. Then, the sum of the series from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \). I converted that into a geometric series and applied the formula. Let me verify that again. Starting index \( a = n+1 \), ending index \( b = 2n \). So, number of terms is \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \). The formula: Sum = \( \frac{r^a - r^{b + 1}}{1 - r} \). So here, \( r = 1/2 \), so: Sum = \( \frac{(1/2)^{n+1} - (1/2)^{2n + 1}}{1 - 1/2} = \frac{(1/2)^{n+1} - (1/2)^{2n + 1}}{1/2} = 2 \times [ (1/2)^{n+1} - (1/2)^{2n + 1} ] = 2 \times (1/2)^{n+1} - 2 \times (1/2)^{2n + 1} = (1/2^n) - (1/2^{2n}) \). Correct. So that part is right. Then, adding the first term \( 1/2^n \), so total norm is \( 1/2^n + 1/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 2/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 1/2^{n-1} - 1/2^{2n} \). That seems correct. Then, taking the limit as \( n \to \infty \). Both terms \( 1/2^{n-1} \) and \( 1/2^{2n} \) go to zero. So, their difference also goes to zero. Therefore, the norm tends to zero, so the convergence is in \( \ell^1 \). Wait, but let me think if there's another perspective here. Maybe there's something about the number of terms increasing? For example, when n increases, the number of non-zero terms in \( y_n \) is increasing. But in the norm calculation, we already accounted for that by summing those terms, which decay exponentially. So even though the number of terms is increasing, each term is getting much smaller, so the total sum still tends to zero. For example, the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( 1/2^k \) is a tail of the geometric series, which is known to be small. Let me see for example, when n = 1, the norm is 1/2^{1-1} - 1/2^{2*1} = 1/1 - 1/4 = 3/4. For n = 2: 1/2^{2-1} - 1/2^{4} = 1/2 - 1/16 = 7/16 ≈ 0.4375 n = 3: 1/4 - 1/64 = 15/64 ≈ 0.234 n = 4: 1/8 - 1/256 = 31/256 ≈ 0.121 n = 5: 1/16 - 1/1024 ≈ 0.0625 - 0.0009766 ≈ 0.0615 So it's decreasing towards zero. So yes, the trend is clear. Alternatively, we can bound the norm: \( \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} < \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \), and since \( 1/2^{n-1} \to 0 \) as \( n \to \infty \), by the squeeze theorem, the norm tends to zero. Therefore, the conclusion is that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). But wait, just to make sure, is there any possibility that I miscalculated the number of terms or the indices? So, y_1 = (1/2, 1/4, 0, 0, ...). Then, x = (1,0,0,...). Then, y1 - x = (-1/2, 1/4, 0, 0, ...). The norm is 1/2 + 1/4 = 3/4, which matches the calculation above. Similarly, for n=2, y_2 = (1 - 1/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). So, y2 = (3/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). Then, y2 - x = (-1/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). The norm is 1/4 + 1/8 + 1/16 = (4 + 2 + 1)/16 = 7/16, which also matches. Therefore, the calculation seems consistent for n=1,2. Hence, my previous steps are correct. The sum of this series is: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1}}{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Adding the first term \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), the total \(\ell^1\)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] As \( n \to \infty \), both \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \) and \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approach zero. Therefore, the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to zero, confirming that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \(\ell^1\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

So, subtracting \( x \) which has all zeros after the first term, the difference \( y_n - x \) is the sequence: \[ \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Now, to find the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of this difference, we need to take the absolute value of each component and sum them up. Since all terms except the first are positive in \( y_n \), the absolute values will just be the terms themselves. Let's compute that: The first term contributes \( \left| -\frac{1}{2^n} \right| = \frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, starting from the second term up to the \( (2n - n + 1) \)-th term? Wait, let's see. The sequence \( y_n \) has non-zero entries from position 1 up to position \( 2n - n + 1 \)? Wait, maybe I need to check how many terms there are. The original \( y_n \) has entries starting from the first component \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), then the next components are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Let's count how many non-zero terms there are after the first component. The exponents in the denominators go from \( n+1 \) up to \( 2n \), inclusive. So, the number of terms is \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \). Wait, let's verify that. If you have exponents from \( n+1 \) to \( 2n \), that's \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \) terms. So, starting from the second component (position 2) up to position \( n + 1 \), there are \( n \) terms each of the form \( \frac{1}{2^{n + k}} \) where \( k \) goes from 1 to \( n \). So, in total, the non-zero entries of \( y_n - x \) are the first component \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \) and then the next \( n \) components \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) up to \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] Now, let's compute this sum. The first term is \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), and then the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( \frac{1}{2^k} \). Let's handle the summation part first. The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a finite geometric series. The general formula for the sum of a geometric series from \( k = a \) to \( k = b \) is \( \frac{r^a - r^{b+1}}{1 - r} \), where \( r \) is the common ratio. Here, \( r = \frac{1}{2} \), so the sum becomes: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1} }{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{ \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} }{ \frac{1}{2} } = 2 \left( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} \right ) = \frac{1}{2^{n}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] So, the summation part simplifies to \( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the total \( \ell^1 \)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right ) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Now, we need to evaluate the limit as \( n \) approaches infinity of this expression. Let's compute each term: First, \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} = 2 \cdot \frac{1}{2^n} \), which clearly approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Second, \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \left( \frac{1}{4} \right)^n \), which also approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the entire expression \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approaches \( 0 - 0 = 0 \). Hence, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), which means that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). Wait a second, let me double-check my steps to make sure I didn't make a mistake. So, starting from the second component (position 2) up to position \( n + 1 \), there are \( n \) terms each of the form \( \frac{1}{2^{n + k}} \) where \( k \) goes from 1 to \( n \). So, in total, the non-zero entries of \( y_n - x \) are the first component \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \) and then the next \( n \) components \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) up to \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] Now, let's compute this sum. The first term is \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), and then the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( \frac{1}{2^k} \). Let's handle the summation part first. The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a finite geometric series. The general formula for the sum of a geometric series from \( k = a \) to \( k = b \) is \( \frac{r^a - r^{b+1}}{1 - r} \), where \( r \) is the common ratio. Here, \( r = \frac{1}{2} \), so the sum becomes: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1} }{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{ \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} }{ \frac{1}{2} } = 2 \left( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} \right ) = \frac{1}{2^{n}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] So, the summation part simplifies to \( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the total \( \ell^1 \)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right ) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Now, we need to evaluate the limit as \( n \) approaches infinity of this expression. Let's compute each term: First, \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} = 2 \cdot \frac{1}{2^n} \), which clearly approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Second, \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \left( \frac{1}{4} \right)^n \), which also approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the entire expression \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approaches \( 0 - 0 = 0 \). Hence, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), which means that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). Wait a second, let me double-check my steps to make sure I didn't make a mistake. Correct. So that part is right. Then, adding the first term \( 1/2^n \), so total norm is \( 1/2^n + 1/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 2/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 1/2^{n-1} - 1/2^{2n} \). That seems correct. Then, taking the limit as \( n \to \infty \). Both terms \( 1/2^{n-1} \) and \( 1/2^{2n} \) go to zero. So, their difference also goes to zero. Therefore, the norm tends to zero, so the convergence is in \( \ell^1 \). Wait, but let me think if there's another perspective here. Maybe there's something about the number of terms increasing? So even though the number of terms is increasing, each term is getting much smaller, so the total sum still tends to zero. Here, \( r = \frac{1}{2} \), so the sum becomes: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1} }{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{ \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} }{ \frac{1}{2} } = 2 \left( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} \right ) = \frac{1}{2^{n}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] So, the summation part simplifies to \( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the total \( \ell^1 \)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right ) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Now, we need to evaluate the limit as \( n \) approaches infinity of this expression. Let's compute each term: First, \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} = 2 \cdot \frac{1}{2^n} \), which clearly approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Second, \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \left( \frac{1}{4} \right)^n \), which also approaches 0 as \( n \to \infty \). Therefore, the entire expression \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approaches \( 0 - 0 = 0 \). Hence, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), which means that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). Wait a second, let me double-check my steps to make sure I didn't make a mistake. So, first, the difference sequence: subtracting \( x = (1, 0, 0, \dots) \) from \( y_n \), which has first term \( 1 - 1/2^n \), so difference first term is \( -1/2^n \). Then, the next terms are \( 1/2^{n+1}, 1/2^{n+2}, ..., 1/2^{2n} \), which are all positive. Then, zeros. So, yes, the norm is the sum of absolute values of these terms. Then, the sum of the series from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \). I converted that into a geometric series and applied the formula. Let me verify that again. Starting index \( a = n+1 \), ending index \( b = 2n \). So, number of terms is \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \). The formula: Sum = \( \frac{r^a - r^{b + 1}}{1 - r} \). So here, \( r = 1/2 \), so: Sum = \( \frac{(1/2)^{n+1} - (1/2)^{2n + 1}}{1 - 1/2} = \frac{(1/2)^{n+1} - (1/2)^{2n + 1}}{1/2} = 2 \times [ (1/2)^{n+1} - (1/2)^{2n + 1} ] = 2 \times (1/2)^{n+1} - 2 \times (1/2)^{2n + 1} = (1/2^n) - (1/2^{2n}) \). Correct. So that part is right. Then, adding the first term \( 1/2^n \), so total norm is \( 1/2^n + 1/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 2/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 1/2^{n-1} - 1/2^{2n} \). That seems correct. Then, taking the limit as \( n \to \infty \). Both terms \( 1/2^{n-1} \) and \( 1/2^{2n} \) go to zero. So, their difference also goes to zero. Therefore, the norm tends to zero, so the convergence is in \( \ell^1 \). Wait, but let me think if there's another perspective here. Maybe there's something about the number of terms increasing? For example, when n increases, the number of non-zero terms in \( y_n \) is increasing. But in the norm calculation, we already accounted for that by summing those terms, which decay exponentially. So even though the number of terms is increasing, each term is getting much smaller, so the total sum still tends to zero. For example, the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( 1/2^k \) is a tail of the geometric series, which is known to be small. Let me see for example, when n = 1, the norm is 1/2^{1-1} - 1/2^{2*1} = 1/1 - 1/4 = 3/4. For n = 2: 1/2^{2-1} - 1/2^{4} = 1/2 - 1/16 = 7/16 ≈ 0.4375 n = 3: 1/4 - 1/64 = 15/64 ≈ 0.234 n = 4: 1/8 - 1/256 = 31/256 ≈ 0.121 n = 5: 1/16 - 1/1024 ≈ 0.0625 - 0.0009766 ≈ 0.0615 So it's decreasing towards zero. So yes, the trend is clear. Alternatively, we can bound the norm: \( \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} < \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \), and since \( 1/2^{n-1} \to 0 \) as \( n \to \infty \), by the squeeze theorem, the norm tends to zero. Correct. So that part is right. Then, adding the first term \( 1/2^n \), so total norm is \( 1/2^n + 1/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 2/2^n - 1/2^{2n} = 1/2^{n-1} - 1/2^{2n} \). That seems correct. Then, taking the limit as \( n \to \infty \). Both terms \( 1/2^{n-1} \) and \( 1/2^{2n} \) go to zero. So, their difference also goes to zero. Therefore, the norm tends to zero, so the convergence is in \( \ell^1 \). Wait, but let me think if there's another perspective here. Maybe there's something about the number of terms increasing? For example, when n increases, the number of non-zero terms in \( y_n \) is increasing. But in the norm calculation, we already accounted for that by summing those terms, which decay exponentially. So even though the number of terms is increasing, each term is getting much smaller, so the total sum still tends to zero. For example, the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( 1/2^k \) is a tail of the geometric series, which is known to be small. First, we compute the difference \( y_n - x \): \[ y_n - x = \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Next, we calculate the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \): \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a geometric series with the first term \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) and the last term \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Problem: Determine whether the sequence \(y_n = \left(1 - \frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}},\dots,\frac{1}{2^{2n}},0,0,0,\dots\right)\) converges in \(\ell^1\) to \((1,0,0,0,\dots)\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

In \( \ell^1 \) space, a sequence \( (y_n) \) converges to \( x \) if the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) goes to zero as \( n \) approaches infinity. First, let's write out what \( y_n - x \) looks like. The first term of \( y_n \) is \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), so subtracting the first term of \( x \), which is 1, gives \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, the next terms of \( y_n \) are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \), followed by zeros. Since all terms except the first are positive in \( y_n \), the absolute values will just be the terms themselves. The general formula for the sum of a geometric series from \( k = a \) to \( k = b \) is \( \frac{r^a - r^{b+1}}{1 - r} \), where \( r \) is the common ratio. For n = 2: 1/2^{2-1} - 1/2^{4} = 1/2 - 1/16 = 7/16 ≈ 0.4375 n = 3: 1/4 - 1/64 = 15/64 ≈ 0.234 n = 4: 1/8 - 1/256 = 31/256 ≈ 0.121 n = 5: 1/16 - 1/1024 ≈ 0.0625 - 0.0009766 ≈ 0.0615 So it's decreasing towards zero. So yes, the trend is clear. Alternatively, we can bound the norm: \( \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} < \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \), and since \( 1/2^{n-1} \to 0 \) as \( n \to \infty \), by the squeeze theorem, the norm tends to zero. Therefore, the conclusion is that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). But wait, just to make sure, is there any possibility that I miscalculated the number of terms or the indices? Let's take n=1 as an example. For n=1, y_1 = (1 - 1/2^1, 1/2^{1+1}, 1/2^{1+2}, ..., 1/2^{2*1}, 0, ...) which is (1 - 1/2, 1/4, 0, 0, ...) because 2n = 2, so up to 1/2^{2} which is 1/4. So, y_1 = (1/2, 1/4, 0, 0, ...). Then, x = (1,0,0,...). Then, y1 - x = (-1/2, 1/4, 0, 0, ...). The norm is 1/2 + 1/4 = 3/4, which matches the calculation above. Similarly, for n=2, y_2 = (1 - 1/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). So, y2 = (3/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). Then, y2 - x = (-1/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). The norm is 1/4 + 1/8 + 1/16 = (4 + 2 + 1)/16 = 7/16, which also matches. Therefore, the calculation seems consistent for n=1,2. Hence, my previous steps are correct. First, we compute the difference \( y_n - x \): \[ y_n - x = \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Next, we calculate the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \): \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a geometric series with the first term \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) and the last term \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). The sum of this series is: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1}}{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Adding the first term \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), the total \(\ell^1\)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] As \( n \to \infty \), both \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \) and \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approach zero. Therefore, the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to zero, confirming that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \(\ell^1\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether the sequence \(y_n = \left(1 - \frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}},\dots,\frac{1}{2^{2n}},0,0,0,\dots\right)\) converges in \(\ell^1\) to \((1,0,0,0,\dots)\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Hmm, let me recall what convergence in \( \ell^1 \) means. In \( \ell^1 \) space, a sequence \( (y_n) \) converges to \( x \) if the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) goes to zero as \( n \) approaches infinity. First, let's write out what \( y_n - x \) looks like. The first term of \( y_n \) is \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), so subtracting the first term of \( x \), which is 1, gives \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, the next terms of \( y_n \) are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \), followed by zeros. So, subtracting \( x \) which has all zeros after the first term, the difference \( y_n - x \) is the sequence: \[ \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Now, to find the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of this difference, we need to take the absolute value of each component and sum them up. Since all terms except the first are positive in \( y_n \), the absolute values will just be the terms themselves. The sequence \( y_n \) has non-zero entries from position 1 up to position \( 2n - n + 1 \)? Wait, maybe I need to check how many terms there are. The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a finite geometric series. The general formula for the sum of a geometric series from \( k = a \) to \( k = b \) is \( \frac{r^a - r^{b+1}}{1 - r} \), where \( r \) is the common ratio. Here, \( r = \frac{1}{2} \), so the sum becomes: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1} }{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{ \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} }{ \frac{1}{2} } = 2 \left( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} \right ) = \frac{1}{2^{n}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] So, the summation part simplifies to \( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the total \( \ell^1 \)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right ) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Now, we need to evaluate the limit as \( n \) approaches infinity of this expression. Similarly, for n=2, y_2 = (1 - 1/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). So, y2 = (3/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). Then, y2 - x = (-1/4, 1/8, 1/16, 0, ...). The norm is 1/4 + 1/8 + 1/16 = (4 + 2 + 1)/16 = 7/16, which also matches. Therefore, the calculation seems consistent for n=1,2. Hence, my previous steps are correct. Therefore, since the norm tends to zero, the sequence \( y_n \) does converge to \( x = (1, 0, 0, ...) \) in \( \ell^1 \). **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether the sequence \( y_n = \left(1 - \frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \) converges in \(\ell^1\) to \( x = (1, 0, 0, 0, \dots) \), we need to check if the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to zero as \( n \) approaches infinity. First, we compute the difference \( y_n - x \): \[ y_n - x = \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Next, we calculate the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \): \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a geometric series with the first term \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) and the last term \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). The sum of this series is: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1}}{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Adding the first term \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), the total \(\ell^1\)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] As \( n \to \infty \), both \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \) and \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approach zero. Therefore, the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to zero, confirming that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \(\ell^1\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether the sequence \(y_n = \left(1 - \frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}},\dots,\frac{1}{2^{2n}},0,0,0,\dots\right)\) converges in \(\ell^1\) to \((1,0,0,0,\dots)\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Hmm, let me recall what convergence in \( \ell^1 \) means. In \( \ell^1 \) space, a sequence \( (y_n) \) converges to \( x \) if the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) goes to zero as \( n \) approaches infinity. First, let's write out what \( y_n - x \) looks like. The first term of \( y_n \) is \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), so subtracting the first term of \( x \), which is 1, gives \( -\frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, the next terms of \( y_n \) are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \), followed by zeros. So, subtracting \( x \) which has all zeros after the first term, the difference \( y_n - x \) is the sequence: \[ \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Now, to find the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of this difference, we need to take the absolute value of each component and sum them up. Since all terms except the first are positive in \( y_n \), the absolute values will just be the terms themselves. Let's compute that: The first term contributes \( \left| -\frac{1}{2^n} \right| = \frac{1}{2^n} \). Then, starting from the second term up to the \( (2n - n + 1) \)-th term? Wait, let's see. The sequence \( y_n \) has non-zero entries from position 1 up to position \( 2n - n + 1 \)? Wait, maybe I need to check how many terms there are. The original \( y_n \) has entries starting from the first component \( 1 - \frac{1}{2^n} \), then the next components are \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Let's count how many non-zero terms there are after the first component. The exponents in the denominators go from \( n+1 \) up to \( 2n \), inclusive. So, the number of terms is \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \). Wait, let's verify that. If you have exponents from \( n+1 \) to \( 2n \), that's \( 2n - (n+1) + 1 = n \) terms. Therefore, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] Now, let's compute this sum. The first term is \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), and then the sum from \( k = n+1 \) to \( 2n \) of \( \frac{1}{2^k} \). Let's handle the summation part first. The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a finite geometric series. The general formula for the sum of a geometric series from \( k = a \) to \( k = b \) is \( \frac{r^a - r^{b+1}}{1 - r} \), where \( r \) is the common ratio. Here, \( r = \frac{1}{2} \), so the sum becomes: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1} }{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{ \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} }{ \frac{1}{2} } = 2 \left( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n + 1}} \right ) = \frac{1}{2^{n}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] So, the summation part simplifies to \( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). Therefore, the total \( \ell^1 \)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right ) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Now, we need to evaluate the limit as \( n \) approaches infinity of this expression. Therefore, the entire expression \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approaches \( 0 - 0 = 0 \). Hence, the \( \ell^1 \)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to 0 as \( n \to \infty \), which means that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \( \ell^1 \). First, we compute the difference \( y_n - x \): \[ y_n - x = \left( -\frac{1}{2^n}, \frac{1}{2^{n+1}}, \frac{1}{2^{n+2}}, \dots, \frac{1}{2^{2n}}, 0, 0, 0, \dots \right) \] Next, we calculate the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \): \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \] The sum \( \sum_{k=n+1}^{2n} \frac{1}{2^k} \) is a geometric series with the first term \( \frac{1}{2^{n+1}} \) and the last term \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \). The sum of this series is: \[ \frac{\left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{n+1} - \left( \frac{1}{2} \right)^{2n + 1}}{1 - \frac{1}{2}} = \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] Adding the first term \( \frac{1}{2^n} \), the total \(\ell^1\)-norm is: \[ \| y_n - x \|_1 = \frac{1}{2^n} + \left( \frac{1}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \right) = \frac{2}{2^n} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} = \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} - \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \] As \( n \to \infty \), both \( \frac{1}{2^{n-1}} \) and \( \frac{1}{2^{2n}} \) approach zero. Therefore, the \(\ell^1\)-norm of \( y_n - x \) tends to zero, confirming that \( y_n \) converges to \( x \) in \(\ell^1\). \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Let \( y = x^2 \), then the denominator becomes \( y^2 + 7y + 1 \). The roots of this quadratic are \( y = \frac{-7 \pm 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \), so the denominator factors as \( (x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2})(x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}) \). Using partial fractions, we decompose the integrand: \[ \frac{1}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} = \frac{B}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{D}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \] Solving for \( B \) and \( D \), we find \( B = -\frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \) and \( D = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \). The integral then splits into two parts: \[ \int_0^\infty \left( \frac{-1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \right) dx \] Each integral is of the form \( \int_0^\infty \frac{dx}{x^2 + c^2} = \frac{\pi}{2c} \). Applying this formula: \[ \frac{-1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} + \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} \] Simplifying the terms, we note that \( \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} = 1 \). Let \( a = \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \) and \( b = \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \). Then \( \sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{5} \). Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} (\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b}) = \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} \cdot \sqrt{5} = \frac{\pi}{6} \] Verification using substitution \( t = x - \frac{1}{x} \) confirms the result. Therefore, the integral evaluates to: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{6}} \]

Let \( a = \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \) and \( b = \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \). Then \( \sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{5} \). Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} (\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b}) = \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} \cdot \sqrt{5} = \frac{\pi}{6} \] Verification using substitution \( t = x - \frac{1}{x} \) confirms the result. Let \( y = x^2 \), then the denominator becomes \( y^2 + 7y + 1 \). The roots of this quadratic are \( y = \frac{-7 \pm 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \), so the denominator factors as \( (x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2})(x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}) \). Using partial fractions, we decompose the integrand: \[ \frac{1}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} = \frac{B}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{D}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \] Solving for \( B \) and \( D \), we find \( B = -\frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \) and \( D = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \). The integral then splits into two parts: \[ \int_0^\infty \left( \frac{-1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \right) dx \] Each integral is of the form \( \int_0^\infty \frac{dx}{x^2 + c^2} = \frac{\pi}{2c} \). Applying this formula: \[ \frac{-1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} + \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} \] Simplifying the terms, we note that \( \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} = 1 \). Let \( a = \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \) and \( b = \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \). Then \( \sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{5} \). Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} (\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b}) = \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} \cdot \sqrt{5} = \frac{\pi}{6} \] Verification using substitution \( t = x - \frac{1}{x} \) confirms the result. Therefore, the integral evaluates to: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{6}} \]

Using partial fractions, we decompose the integrand: \[ \frac{1}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} = \frac{B}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{D}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \] Solving for \( B \) and \( D \), we find \( B = -\frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \) and \( D = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \). Let \( a = \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \) and \( b = \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \). Then \( \sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{5} \). Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} (\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b}) = \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} \cdot \sqrt{5} = \frac{\pi}{6} \] Verification using substitution \( t = x - \frac{1}{x} \) confirms the result. Let \( y = x^2 \), then the denominator becomes \( y^2 + 7y + 1 \). The roots of this quadratic are \( y = \frac{-7 \pm 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \), so the denominator factors as \( (x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2})(x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}) \). Using partial fractions, we decompose the integrand: \[ \frac{1}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} = \frac{B}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{D}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \] Solving for \( B \) and \( D \), we find \( B = -\frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \) and \( D = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \). The integral then splits into two parts: \[ \int_0^\infty \left( \frac{-1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \right) dx \] Each integral is of the form \( \int_0^\infty \frac{dx}{x^2 + c^2} = \frac{\pi}{2c} \). Applying this formula: \[ \frac{-1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} + \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} \] Simplifying the terms, we note that \( \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} = 1 \). Let \( a = \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \) and \( b = \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \). Then \( \sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{5} \). Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} (\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b}) = \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} \cdot \sqrt{5} = \frac{\pi}{6} \] Verification using substitution \( t = x - \frac{1}{x} \) confirms the result. Therefore, the integral evaluates to: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{6}} \]

Therefore, the integrand becomes: dt/(t² +9). Therefore, integrating from t=-infty to t=infty. Therefore, ∫_{-infty}^infty dt/(t² +9)= π/3. Therefore, 2I=π/3 => I=π/6. Which matches the previous result. So that's a nice verification. Therefore, the integral is π/6. So the answer is π divided by 6. Therefore, boxed, it's \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{6}}. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{6}} </think> To evaluate the integral \( u = \int_0^\infty \frac{dx}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} \), we start by factoring the denominator. Let \( y = x^2 \), then the denominator becomes \( y^2 + 7y + 1 \). The roots of this quadratic are \( y = \frac{-7 \pm 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \), so the denominator factors as \( (x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2})(x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}) \). Using partial fractions, we decompose the integrand: \[ \frac{1}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} = \frac{B}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{D}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \] Solving for \( B \) and \( D \), we find \( B = -\frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \) and \( D = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \). The integral then splits into two parts: \[ \int_0^\infty \left( \frac{-1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{1/(3\sqrt{5})}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \right) dx \] Each integral is of the form \( \int_0^\infty \frac{dx}{x^2 + c^2} = \frac{\pi}{2c} \). Applying this formula: \[ \frac{-1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} + \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2 \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}}} \] Simplifying the terms, we note that \( \sqrt{\frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} = 1 \). Let \( a = \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \) and \( b = \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2} \). Then \( \sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b} = \sqrt{5} \). Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} (\sqrt{a} - \sqrt{b}) = \frac{\pi}{6\sqrt{5}} \cdot \sqrt{5} = \frac{\pi}{6} \] Verification using substitution \( t = x - \frac{1}{x} \) confirms the result. Therefore, the integral evaluates to: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{6}} \]

Using partial fractions, we decompose the integrand: \[ \frac{1}{x^4 + 7x^2 + 1} = \frac{B}{x^2 + \frac{7 + 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} + \frac{D}{x^2 + \frac{7 - 3\sqrt{5}}{2}} \] Solving for \( B \) and \( D \), we find \( B = -\frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \) and \( D = \frac{1}{3\sqrt{5}} \).
